                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The draft genome of watermelon ( Citrullus lanatus ) and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2012 45:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2012-11-25"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2012 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Zhangjun Fei and colleagues report the draft genome of a Chinese elite watermelon inbred line 97103 and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions that represent the three subspecies of Citrullus lunatus. Comparative genome-wide analyses identify the extent of genetic diversity and population structure of watermelon germplasm. Watermelon, Citrullus lanatus, is an important cucurbit crop grown throughout the world. Here we report a high-quality draft genome sequence of the east Asia watermelon cultivar 97103 (2n = 2&#215; = 22) containing 23,440 predicted protein-coding genes. Comparative genomics analysis provided an evolutionary scenario for the origin of the 11 watermelon chromosomes derived from a 7-chromosome paleohexaploid eudicot ancestor. Resequencing of 20 watermelon accessions representing three different C. lanatus subspecies produced numerous haplotypes and identified the extent of genetic diversity and population structure of watermelon germplasm. Genomic regions that were preferentially selected during domestication were identified. Many disease-resistance genes were also found to be lost during domestication. In addition, integrative genomic and transcriptomic analyses yielded important insights into aspects of phloem-based vascular signaling in common between watermelon and cucumber and identified genes crucial to valuable fruit-quality traits, including sugar accumulation and citrulline metabolism."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Watermelon, <i>Citrullus lanatus</i>, is an important cucurbit crop grown throughout the world. Here we report a high-quality draft genome sequence of the east Asia watermelon cultivar 97103 (2<i>n</i> = 2Ã = 22) containing 23,440 predicted protein-coding genes. Comparative genomics analysis provided an evolutionary scenario for the origin of the 11 watermelon chromosomes derived from a 7-chromosome paleohexaploid eudicot ancestor. Resequencing of 20 watermelon accessions representing three different <i>C. lanatus</i> subspecies produced numerous haplotypes and identified the extent of genetic diversity and population structure of watermelon germplasm. Genomic regions that were preferentially selected during domestication were identified. Many disease-resistance genes were also found to be lost during domestication. In addition, integrative genomic and transcriptomic analyses yielded important insights into aspects of phloem-based vascular signaling in common between watermelon and cucumber and identified genes crucial to valuable fruit-quality traits, including sugar accumulation and citrulline metabolism.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Watermelon (<i>C. lanatus</i>) is an important cucurbit crop, accounting for 7% of the worldwide area devoted to vegetable production. The annual world production of watermelon is about 90 million tons, making it among the top five most consumed fresh fruits (<a href="http://faostat.fao.org/">http://faostat.fao.org/</a>). Watermelon belongs to the xerophytic genus <i>Citrullus</i> Schrad. ex Eckl. et Zeyh. of the botanical family <i>Cucurbitaceae</i>. The center of diversity and possible center of origin of <i>Citrullus</i> is southern Africa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Erickson, D.L., Smith, B.D., Clarke, A.C., Sandweiss, D.H. &amp; Tuross, N. An Asian origin for a 10,000-year-old domesticated plant in the Americas. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 102, 18315â18320 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1356">1</a></sup>. <i>C. lanatus</i> includes three subspecies: <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>lanatus</i>, which represents a group of ancient cultigens, the 'tsamma' or 'citron' watermelon, that naturally thrives in southern Africa; <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i>, which represents the egusi watermelon group that has large edible seeds with a fleshy pericarp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Fursa, T.B. On the taxonomy of the genus Citrullus Schad. Bot. Zh. 57, 31â34 (1972)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1376">2</a></sup>; and <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i>, which represents the sweet (dessert) watermelon group that gave rise to the modern cultivated watermelon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Jeffrey, C. Cucurbitaceae. in Mansfeld's Encyclopedia of Agricultural and Horticultural Crops (ed. Hanelt, P.) Vol. 3, 1510â1557 (Springer, 2001)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1386">3</a></sup>.</p><p>The large edible watermelon fruits contribute to the diets of consumers throughout the world. Although comprised mainly of water (often over 90%), watermelon also contains important nutritional compounds, including sugars, lycopene and cardiovascular healthâpromoting amino acids, such as citrulline, arginine and glutathione<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Hayashi, T. et al. L-citrulline and L-arginine supplementation retards the progression of high-cholesterol-dietâinduced atherosclerosis in rabbits. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 102, 13681â13686 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1393">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Collins, J.K. et al. Watermelon consumption increases plasma arginine concentrations in adults. Nutrition 23, 261â266 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1396">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Perkins-Veazie, P., Collins, J.K., Davis, A.R. &amp; Roberts, W. Carotenoid content of 50 watermelon cultivars. J. Agric. Food Chem. 54, 2593â2597 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1399">6</a></sup>. Watermelon and cucurbit species in general have unique developmental mechanisms that facilitate the rapid growth and formation of giant pepo fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Schaefer, H. &amp; Renner, S.S. Phylogenetic relationships in the order Cucurbitales and a new classification of the gourd family (Cucurbitaceae). Taxon 60, 122â138 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1403">7</a></sup>. Fruits of modern watermelon varieties are diverse in shape, size, color, texture, flavor and nutrient composition. However, years of cultivation and selection targeting yield and desirable fruit qualities have narrowed the genetic base of watermelon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Levi, A., Thomas, C.E., Wehner, T.C. &amp; Zhang, X. Low genetic diversity indicated the need to broaden the genetic base of cultivated watermelon. HortScience 36, 1096â1101 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1407">8</a></sup>, resulting in a major bottleneck in watermelon improvement.</p><p>Knowledge of genome sequences is indispensable for basic biological research and crop improvement. Here we report a high-quality genome sequence of an east Asia watermelon cultivar, 97103 (2<i>n</i> = 2Ã = 22), and resequencing of 20 watermelon accessions spanning the genetic diversity of <i>C. lanatus</i>. Our comprehensive genomic and transcriptome analyses provide insights into the structure and evolution of the watermelon genome, the genetic diversity and structure of watermelon populations and the molecular mechanisms of important biological processes such as fruit quality and phloem-based vascular signaling. Together, these results will assist in identifying and accessing the plethora of watermelon genetic diversity that remains to be tapped for biological discovery and crop improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We selected the Chinese elite watermelon inbred line 97103 for genome sequencing. We generated a total of 46.18 Gb of high-quality genomic sequence using Illumina sequencing technology (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 1</a>), representing 108.6-fold coverage of the entire watermelon genome, which has an estimated genome size of <span class="stix">â¼</span>425 Mb on the basis of our 17-mer depth distribution analysis of the sequenced reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>) and an earlier flow cytometry analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E.D. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 208â218 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1438">9</a></sup>. <i>De novo</i> assembly of the Illumina reads resulted in a final assembly of 353.5 Mb, representing 83.2% of the watermelon genome. The assembly consists of 1,793 scaffolds (â¥500 bp) with N50 lengths of 2.38 Mb and 26.38 kb for the scaffolds and contigs, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>). A total of 234 scaffolds covering approximately 330 Mb (93.5% of the assembled genome) were anchored to the 11 watermelon chromosomes, among which 126 and 94 scaffolds accounting for 70% and 65% of the assembled genome were ordered and oriented, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ren, Y. et al. A high resolution genetic map anchoring scaffolds of the sequenced watermelon genome. PLoS ONE 7, e29453 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1449">10</a></sup>.</p><p>We sought to determine why 16.8% of the genome was not covered by our genome assembly by aligning unassembled reads (17.4% of the total reads) to the assembled genome with less stringent criteria (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 3</a>). We found that the unassembled genome regions are composed primarily of sequences that are similar to those of the assembled regions. Distribution of the unassembled reads on the watermelon chromosomes showed the same pattern as that for transposable elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). We identified three major repeat units from the unassembled sequences on the basis of their substantial read depths and sequence similarities to centromeres, telomeres and ribosomal DNA (rDNA) clusters. We further confirmed the nature of these repeats by FISH (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#Fig1">Fig. 1bâd</a>). Together these results support the notion that underestimation of the repeat proportion has an important role in the unassembled component of <i>de novo</i> genome assemblies, especially those generated using next-generation sequencing technologies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101â108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1475">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1478">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Al-Dous, E.K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1481">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275â1281 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1484">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109â116 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1487">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1490">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83â89 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1494">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Dassanayake, M. et al. The genome of the extremophile crucifer Thellungiella parvula. Nat. Genet. 43, 913â918 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1497">18</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Distribution of unassembled reads on chromosome 1 and FISH patterns of probes from three repeat units related to the centromere, telomere and 45S rDNA clusters."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Distribution of unassembled reads on chromosome 1 and FISH patterns of probes from three repeat units related to the centromere, telomere and 45S rDNA clusters.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2470/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2470/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2470_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2470/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2470_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="353"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Distribution of unassembled reads on chromosome 1. The distribution of unassembled reads on the other ten chromosomes is shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Figure 2</a>. TEs, transposable elements. (<b>b</b>) FISH of watermelon chromosomes stained with 4â²-6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (DAPI, blue) using probes from repeat units similar to the centromere (pink). (<b>c</b>) FISH using probes from repeat units similar to the telomere (red). (<b>d</b>) FISH using probes from repeat units similar to the rDNAs (green, 45S; pink, 5S). Scale bars, 5 Î¼m.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2470/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We further evaluated the quality of the assembled watermelon genome using approximately one million ESTs, four completely sequenced BACs and paired-end sequences of 667 BAC clones. Our analyses supported the high quality of the watermelon genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 4â6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Figs. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">4</a>), which is favorably comparable to several other recently published plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101â108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1551">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1554">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Al-Dous, E.K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1557">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275â1281 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1560">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109â116 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1563">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1566">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83â89 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1570">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Dassanayake, M. et al. The genome of the extremophile crucifer Thellungiella parvula. Nat. Genet. 43, 913â918 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d36135e1573">18</a></sup> using next-generation sequencing technologies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#Tab1">Table 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Comparison of watermelon genome assembly with other plant genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2470/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat sequence annotation and gene prediction</h3><p>Transposable elements are major components of eukaryotic genomes. We identified a total of 159.8 Mb (45.2%) of the assembled watermelon genome as transposable element repeats. Among these repeats, 68.3% could be annotated with known repeat families. The long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons, mainly Gypsy-type and Copia-type LTRs, are predominant. The distribution of transposable element divergence rates showed a peak at 32% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). We further identified 920 (7.8 Mb) full-length LTR retrotransposons in the watermelon genome. We found that over the past 4.5 million years, LTR retrotransposons accumulated much faster in watermelon than in cucumber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275â1281 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2028">14</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>) such that the overall difference in their genome sizes may reflect the differential LTR retrotransposon accumulation.</p><p>We predicted 23,440 high-confidence protein-coding genes in the watermelon genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 7</a>), which is close to the number of genes predicted in the cucumber genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, Z. et al. RNA-Seq improves annotation of protein-coding genes in the cucumber genome. BMC Genomics 12, 540 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2041">19</a></sup>. Approximately 85% of the watermelon predicted genes had either known homologs or could be functionally classified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 8</a>). In addition, we also identified 123 ribosomal RNA (rRNA), 789 transfer RNA, 335 small nuclear RNA and 141 microRNA genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><p>In accordance with previously reported plant genomes, the watermelon protein-coding genes showed a clear enrichment pattern within subtelomeric regions. In contrast, the transposable elementârelated fraction of the genome was located primarily within the pericentromeric and centromeric regions. The short arms of chromosomes 4, 8 and 11 are highly enriched with repeat sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). The 97103 genome contained one 5S and two 45S rDNA clusters on the short arm of chromosomes 4 and 8 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ren, Y. et al. A high resolution genetic map anchoring scaffolds of the sequenced watermelon genome. PLoS ONE 7, e29453 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2056">10</a>). Using FISH, we further investigated rDNA patterns in genomes of 20 representative watermelon accessions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a>). The number and location of 5S and 45S rDNA sites in the genomes of the ten modern cultivated (<i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i>) and six semiwild watermelon (<i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i>) were identical to those in the 97103 genome, whereas the genomes of the four more distantly related wild watermelon (<i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>lanatus</i>) contained one 45S and two 5S rDNA sites, with the additional 5S rDNA site on the short arm of chromosome 11 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). These results indicate that chromosome fusion, fission and transposition of rDNA might occur during the evolution of <i>C. lanatus</i> species. Our analysis also confirmed the phylogenetic relationship of these three watermelon subspecies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Dane, F. &amp; Liu, J. Diversity and origin of cultivated and citron type watermelon (Citrullus lanatus). Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 54, 1255â1265 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2089">20</a></sup> and supported the hypothesis that <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i> is the recent ancestor of <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Cucurbit genome evolution</h3><p>Genome-wide duplication in angiosperms is common and represents an important molecular mechanism that has shaped modern plant karyotypes. In the watermelon genome, we identified seven major triplications that corresponded to 302 paralogous relationships covering 29% of the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). These ancestral triplicates corresponded to the shared paleohexaploidization event (referenced as Î³) reported for eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2116">21</a></sup> that dates back to 76â130 million years ago. This would be well in advance of the cucurbit genome speciation event that occurred 15â23 million years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genome synteny, duplication patterns and evolutionary history of watermelon, cucumber and melon."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Genome synteny, duplication patterns and evolutionary history of watermelon, cucumber and melon.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2470/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2470/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2470_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2470/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2470_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="606"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Schematic representation of paralogous pairs identified within the watermelon genome (chromosomes w1âw11). Each line represents a syntenic region. Different colors reflect origin from the seven ancestral eudicot chromosome karyotype (A1, A4, A7, A10, A13, A16 and A19). (<b>b</b>) Schematic representation of syntenies among watermelon (chromosomes 1â11), cucumber (chromosomes 1â7) and melon genomes (linkage groups (LG) 1â12). Each line represents a syntenic region. Shared synteny between two of the three species is linked by a light gray line. (<b>c</b>) Evolution of the watermelon genome (w1âw11 at the bottom) from the common eudicot genome ancestors of seven chromosomes (A1, A4, A7, A10, A13, A16 and A19) and the derived paleohexaploid <i>n</i> = 21 (A1âA21) ancestor intermediate. Colored blocks represent the evolution of segments from the 7- or 21-chromosome ancestors to reach the modern watermelon genome structure. The 172 chromosomal fusions and fissions are highlighted with colored arrows. TE, transposable element; WGD, whole-genome duplication.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2470/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To access the nature of evolutionary events leading to modern cucurbit genome structures, we analyzed the syntenic relationships between watermelon, cucumber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, Z. et al. RNA-Seq improves annotation of protein-coding genes in the cucumber genome. BMC Genomics 12, 540 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2158">19</a></sup>, melon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Deleu, W. et al. A set of EST-SNPs for map saturation and cultivar identification in melon. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 90 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2162">22</a></sup> and grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2166">21</a></sup>. We chose grape as the reference, as it is known to be the closest relative to the eudicot ancestor structured in seven protochromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Salse, J. In silico archeogenomics unveils modern plant genome organisation, regulation and evolution. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 15, 122â130 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2170">23</a></sup>. We identified a total of 3,543 orthologous relationships covering 60% of the watermelon genome. We then investigated the detailed chromosome-to-chromosome relationships within the <i>Cucurbitaceae</i> family and identified orthologous chromosomes between watermelon, cucumber and melon (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). The complicated syntenic patterns illustrated as mosaic chromosome-to-chromosome orthologous relationships unveiled a high degree of complexity of chromosomal evolution and rearrangement among these three important crop species of the <i>Cucurbitaceae</i> family.</p><p>Integration of independent analyses of duplications within, and syntenies between, the four eudicot genomes (watermelon, cucumber, melon and grape) led to the precise characterization in watermelon of the seven paleotriplications identified recently as the basis for the definition of seven ancestral chromosomal groups in eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Abrouk, M. et al. Palaeogenomics of plants: synteny-based modelling of extinct ancestors. Trends Plant Sci. 15, 479â487 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2187">24</a></sup>. On the basis of the ancestral hexaploidization (Î³) reported for the eudicots, we propose an evolutionary scenario that has shaped the 11 watermelon chromosomes from the 7-chromosome eudicot ancestors through the 21 paleohexaploid intermediates. We suggest that the transition from the 21-chromosome eudicot intermediate ancestors involved 81 fissions and 91 fusions to reach the modern 11-chromosome structure of watermelon, which is represented as a mosaic of 102 ancestral blocks (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Assessment of genetic diversity in watermelon germplasm</h3><p>We selected 20 representative watermelon accessions for genome resequencing. These included ten cultivated accessions representing the major varieties of <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i> (five east Asia and five America ecotypes), six semiwild <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i> and four wild <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>lanatus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). We sequenced these accessions to between 5Ã and 16Ã coverage and mapped the short reads to the genome of 97103 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 11</a>). We identified a total of 6,784,860 candidate SNPs and 965,006 small insertions/deletions (indels) among the 20 resequenced lines and 97103. The major variations existed between <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>lanatus</i> and the other two subspecies, whereas the variation within the cultivated watermelon, especially <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i> America ecotype, was relatively low (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 12</a>). The accuracies of our SNP and indel calling were 99.3% and 98%, respectively, as indicated by Sanger sequencing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 13</a>). This extensive watermelon genome variation dataset, covering a wide spectrum of watermelon genetic diversity, represents a valuable resource for biological discovery and germplasm improvement.</p><p>We evaluated the genetic diversity of the watermelon population using two common summary statistics, <i>Ï</i> and <i>Î¸w</i> values<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Tajima, F. Evolutionary relationship of DNA sequences in finite populations. Genetics 105, 437â460 (1983)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2261">25</a></sup>. The estimated amount of diversity in watermelon (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 14</a>) was substantially lower than that found in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Lai, J. et al. Genome-wide patterns of genetic variation among elite maize inbred lines. Nat. Genet. 42, 1027â1030 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2268">26</a></sup>, soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Lam, H.M. et al. Resequencing of 31 wild and cultivated soybean genomes identifies patterns of genetic diversity and selection. Nat. Genet. 42, 1053â1059 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2273">27</a></sup> and rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Xu, X. et al. Resequencing 50 accessions of cultivated and wild rice yields markers for identifying agronomically important genes. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 105â111 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2277">28</a></sup>. Wild watermelon contains greater genetic diversity, indicating additional genetic opportunity for watermelon improvement. We also investigated the population structure and relationships among the watermelon accessions through construction of a neighbor-joining tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>) and principal component analysis (PCA) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). Both analyses indicated the close relationship between <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i> and <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). Additional analysis of population structure using the FRAPPE program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Tang, H., Peng, J., Wang, P. &amp; Risch, N.J. Estimation of individual admixture: analytical and study design considerations. Genet. Epidemiol. 28, 289â301 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2303">29</a></sup> with <i>K</i> (the number of populations) set from 2 to 5 identified a new subgroup within the <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i> group (when <i>K</i> = 5) and admixtures between <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i> and <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). The new subgroup shows some characteristics of the cultivated watermelon, such as soft flesh texture, pink flesh color and relatively high sugar content (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Together these results offer further support for our proposed evolutionary scenario of <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i> to <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i> derived from the FISH analysis of chromosomal rDNA distribution.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Population structure of watermelon accessions."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Population structure of watermelon accessions.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2470/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2470/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2470_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2470/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2470_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="949"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree of watermelon accessions on the basis of SNPs. (<b>b</b>) PCA analysis of watermelon accessions using SNPs as markers. <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i> east Asia and America ecotypes and <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i> accessions cluster together (arrow) and are almost indistinguishable. (<b>c</b>) Population structure of watermelon accessions. Each color represents one population, each accession is represented by a vertical bar, and the length of each colored segment in each vertical bar represents the proportion contributed by ancestral populations. Shown are representative watermelon pictures from each subspecies or ecotype.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2470/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We next scanned the genome for regions with the highest differences of genetic diversity (<i>Ï</i><sub><i>mucosospermus</i></sub>/<i>Ï</i><sub><i>vulgaris</i></sub>) between <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i> and <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i>. These regions represent potential selective sweeps during watermelon domestication, as modern watermelon cultivars are thought to have been domesticated from <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i>. We identified a total of 108 regions (7.78 Mb in size) containing 741 candidate genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#Fig4">Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 15</a>). Although gene complements in these regions could have been affected by genetic hitchhiking, we identified biological processes significantly enriched in candidate genes that were related to important selected traits when compared to the whole genome, including regulation of carbohydrate use, sugar-mediated signaling, carbohydrate metabolism, response to sucrose stimulus, regulation of nitrogen-compound metabolism, cellular response to nitrogen starvation and growth (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 16â18</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Diversity (Ï) distribution for C. lanatus subsp. vulgaris (blue) and C. lanatus subsp. mucosospermus (red) across the 11 watermelon chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Diversity (<i>Ï</i>) distribution for <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i> (blue) and <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i> (red) across the 11 watermelon chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2470/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2470/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2470_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2470/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2470_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1270"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Pink bars below the <i>x</i> axis correspond to regions with 1% significance level of diversity difference (<i>Ï</i><sub><i>mucosospermus</i></sub>/<i>Ï</i><sub><i>vulgaris</i></sub>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2470/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>It is noteworthy that certain noncentromeric regions, especially a large region on chromosome 3 (from <span class="stix">â¼</span>3.4 Mb to <span class="stix">â¼</span>5.6 Mb), have particularly high nucleotide divergence only among <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i> accessions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). A previous report described a similar finding in three different rice crosses, and it was suggested that these population-specific high-divergence regions were highly associated with genes involved in reproductive barriers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Harushima, Y. et al. Diverse variation of reproductive barriers in three intraspecific rice crosses. Genetics 160, 313â322 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2510">30</a></sup>. We analyzed genes in the large high-diversity region on chromosome 3 and, indeed, found that the most significantly enriched gene categories were recognition of pollen and the pollen-pistil interaction; both of these gene categories are related to reproductive barriers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 19</a>). In addition, we determined that the region contained a large cluster of 12 tandemly arrayed S-locus protein kinase genes, which are involved in reproductive barriers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Nasrallah, J.B. &amp; Nasrallah, M.E. Pollen-stigma signaling in the sporophytic self-incompatibility response. Plant Cell 5, 1325â1335 (1993)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2518">31</a></sup>. The high nucleotide divergence of reproductive barrier genes in <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i>, the recent progenitor of modern cultivated watermelon, indicates that the domestication of watermelon could be a possible force responsible for the rapid evolution of reproductive barriers, as has been reported in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Harushima, Y. et al. Diverse variation of reproductive barriers in three intraspecific rice crosses. Genetics 160, 313â322 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2528">30</a></sup>. Furthermore, genes involved in plant responses to abiotic and biotic stresses were also significantly enriched in this region, in addition to genes related to several known selected traits such as carbohydrate metabolism, fruit flavor (terpene metabolism) and seed oil content (fatty acid metabolism) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 19</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Evolution of disease resistance genes in watermelon</h3><p>The watermelon crop suffers major losses from numerous diseases. Therefore, improvement in pathogen resistance is an ongoing objective of watermelon breeding programs. To investigate the molecular basis for pathogen susceptibility, we searched for three major classes of resistance genes in the watermelon genome, namely the nucleotide-binding site and leucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR), lipoxygenase (LOX)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Feussner, I. &amp; Wasternack, C. The lipoxygenase pathway. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 53, 275â297 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2544">32</a></sup> and receptor-like gene families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Fritz-Laylin, L.K., Krishnamurthy, N., Tor, M., Sjolander, K.V. &amp; Jones, J.D. Phylogenomic analysis of the receptor-like proteins of rice and Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 138, 611â623 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2548">33</a></sup>. We identified a total of 44 NBS-LRR genes, including 18 Toll interleukin receptor (TIR)-NBS-LRRâ and 26 coiled-coil (CC)-NBS-LRRâencoding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 20</a>). The watermelon NBS-LRR genes evolved independently, and we detected no sequence exchanges between different homologs. Such evolutionary patterns are similar to those of type II R genes in lettuce and <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Kuang, H. et al. Multiple genetic processes result in heterogeneous rates of evolution within the major cluster disease resistance genes in lettuce. Plant Cell 16, 2870â2894 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2557">34</a></sup>, indicating that watermelon has low diversity of NBS-LRR genes. The number of NBS-LRR genes in the watermelon genome is similar to that in cucumber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275â1281 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2561">14</a></sup> and papaya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Ming, R. et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452, 991â996 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2566">35</a></sup> but is considerably fewer than that in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Schnable, P.S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112â1115 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2570">36</a></sup>, rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Yu, J. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296, 79â92 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2574">37</a></sup> and apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2578">12</a></sup>. In contrast, the LOX gene family has undergone an expansion in the watermelon genome with 26 members, 19 of which are arranged in two tandem gene arrays (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). Similar findings have been reported in cucumber, with expansion of the LOX gene family having been considered as a possible complementary mechanism to cope with pathogen invasion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275â1281 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2585">14</a></sup>. We further identified 197 receptor-like genes in the watermelon genome, among which 35 encode receptor-like proteins lacking a kinase domain and 162 encode receptor-like kinases that have an intracellular kinase domain in addition to the extracellular LRR and transmembrane domains (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 20</a>). Many of these resistance genes are located on chromosomes in clusters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>), suggesting tandem duplications as their evolutionary basis.</p><p>It has been speculated that the lack of resistance to a wide range of diseases in modern watermelon cultivars is the result of the many years of cultivation and selection that have focused on desirable fruit qualities at the expense of disease resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Levi, A., Thomas, C.E., Wehner, T.C. &amp; Zhang, X. Low genetic diversity indicated the need to broaden the genetic base of cultivated watermelon. HortScience 36, 1096â1101 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2599">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Harris, K.R., Wechter, W.P. &amp; Levi, A. Isolation, sequence analysis, and linkage mapping of NBS-LRR disease resistance gene analogs in Watermelon. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 134, 649â657 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2602">38</a></sup>. To test this notion, we performed <i>de novo</i> assemblies of unmapped reads pooled each from modern cultivated (<i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i>) and semiwild and wild (<i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i> and <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>lanatus</i>, respectively) accessions. We identified 11 and 69 genes from the cultivated and the semiwild and wild groups, respectively, that are homologous to known plant proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 21</a>). It is worth mentioning here that the 69 new genes identified from the semiwild and wild group were highly enriched with disease-related genes including, 6 TIR-LRR-NBS genes, 1 PR-1 gene and 3 lipoxygenase genes, whereas none of the 11 genes identified in the cultivated group were disease related. In addition, all of the 44 NBS-LRR genes identified in the 97103 genome were also present in the semiwild and wild accessions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). These findings support the hypothesis that a large portion of disease resistance genes has been lost during watermelon domestication.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Analysis of cucurbit phloem sap and vascular transcriptomes</h3><p>The angiosperm enucleate sieve tube system contains mRNA, some of which has been shown to function as a long-distance signaling agent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Kim, M., Canio, W., Kessler, S. &amp; Sinha, N. Developmental changes due to long-distance movement of a homeobox fusion transcript in tomato. Science 293, 287â289 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2642">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kehr, J. &amp; Buhtz, A. Long distance transport and movement of RNA through the phloem. J. Exp. Bot. 59, 85â92 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2645">40</a></sup>. Through deep transcriptome sequencing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 22</a>), we identified 13,775 and 14,242 mRNA species in watermelon and cucumber vascular bundles, respectively, and 1,519 and 1,012 transcripts in the watermelon and cucumber phloem sap, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 23â26</a>). Notably, we found that the gene sets in the vascular bundles between the two cucurbit species were almost identical, whereas only 50â60% of the transcripts detected in the phloem sap were common between the two species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 27</a>). Gene Ontology (GO) term enrichment analysis indicated that the major categories among the common phloem transcripts were response to stress or stimulus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 28</a>), which is fully consistent with the central role of the plant vascular system, and the phloem in particular, in the long-distance communication system that integrates abiotic and biotic stress signaling at the whole-plant level<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Lough, T.J. &amp; Lucas, W.J. Integrative plant biology: role of phloem long-distance macromolecular trafficking. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 57, 203â232 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2665">41</a></sup>. In contrast, analysis of the phloem transcripts that are unique to watermelon identified macromolecular biosynthesis process and protein metabolic process as the major GO categories (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 29</a>). The unique phloem sap transcripts may reflect specialized functions that are unique to the role of the phloem in these species. It is noteworthy that the watermelon phloem contained 118 transcription factors, whereas we identified only 46 transcription factors in cucumber and 32 transcription factors that were common to both (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 30â32</a>).</p><p>Pumpkin (<i>Cucurbita maxima</i>) has been used as a model system for phloem studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Yoo, B.C. et al. A systemic small RNA signaling system in plants. Plant Cell 16, 1979â2000 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2681">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ham, B.K. et al. A polypyrimidine tract binding protein, pumpkin RBP50, forms the basis of a phloem-mobile ribonucleoprotein complex. Plant Cell 21, 197â215 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2684">43</a></sup>. We developed pumpkin vascular bundle and phloem sap transcript catalogs through generation and <i>de novo</i> assembly of the Illumina paired-end RNA sequencing (RNA-Seq) reads. Comparative analysis of the watermelon, cucumber and pumpkin phloem transcriptomes indicated that approximately 36% of their transcripts were in common (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). These conserved transcripts probably carry out functions that are central to the operation of the sieve tube system in most cucurbit and possibly additional species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Regulation of watermelon fruit development and quality</h3><p>Watermelon fruit development is a complex process involving major changes in size, color, texture, sugar content and nutritional components. To obtain a comprehensive characterization of the genes involved in the development and quality of watermelon fruit, we performed strand-specific RNA-Seq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Zhong, S. et al. High-throughput Illumina strand-specific RNA sequencing library preparation. Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. 8, 940â949 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2702">44</a></sup> of both the flesh and rind at four crucial stages of fruit development in the inbred line 97103 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 33</a>). We identified 3,046 and 558 genes that were differentially expressed in the flesh and rind, respectively, during fruit development and 5,352 genes that were differentially expressed between the flesh and rind in at least one of the four stages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 34â36</a>). GO term enrichment analysis indicated that during fruit development in both the flesh and rind, biological processes such as cell-wall biogenesis, flavonoid metabolism and defense responses were significantly altered (false discovery rates (FDR) &lt; 0.01), whereas carotenoid, hexose and monosaccharide metabolic processes were only significantly altered in the flesh, supporting major physiological differences, including sugar content and fruit color, between the flesh and rind (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 37</a>).</p><p>Sugar content is a key factor in determining watermelon fruit quality. The sweetness of a watermelon is determined by both the total sugar content and the ratios among the major accumulated sugars: glucose, fructose and sucrose<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Brown, A.C. &amp; Summers, W.L. Carbohydrate accumulation and color development in watermelon. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 110, 683â686 (1985)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2718">45</a></sup>. In young 97103 fruit flesh, fructose and glucose are the predominant sugars, whereas in mature 97103 fruit flesh, both sucrose and total sugar content are substantially increased, with sucrose then becoming the dominant sugar; in the rind, the sugar content remains relatively lows (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 38</a>). Final sugar accumulation in watermelon fruit is determined by sugar unloading from the phloem followed by uptake and metabolism within the fruit flesh. The annotated watermelon genome contains a total of 62 sugar metabolic enzyme genes and 76 sugar transporter genes, among which 13 sugar metabolic genes and 14 sugar transporter genes were differentially expressed during flesh development and between the flesh and rind tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 39</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">40</a>). On the basis of these results and prior published work from other plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Slewinski, T.L. Diverse functional roles of monosaccharide transporters and their homologs in vascular plants: a physiological perspective. Mol. Plant 4, 641â662 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2731">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Dai, N. et al. Metabolism of soluble sugars in developing melon fruit: a global transcriptional view of the metabolic transition to sucrose accumulation. Plant Mol. Biol. 76, 1â18 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2734">47</a></sup>, we propose a model for sugar metabolism in the cells of watermelon fruit flesh (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). Specifically, during watermelon flesh development, Î±-galactosidase, insoluble acid invertase, neutral invertase, sucrose phosphate synthase, UDP-glucose 4-epimerase, soluble acid invertase and UDP-galactose/glucose pyrophosphorylase function as key enzymes involved in regulating sugar unloading and metabolism. Furthermore, the 14 differentially expressed sugar transporters are probably responsible for sugar partitioning (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>Transcription factors also have a role in sugar accumulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Slewinski, T.L. &amp; Braun, D.M. Current perspectives on the regulation of whole-plant carbohydrate partitioning. Plant Sci. 178, 341â349 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2748">48</a></sup>. Of the 1,448 putative transcription factor genes identified in the watermelon genome, 193 showed significant expression changes (FDR &lt; 0.01) during flesh development and also in flesh compared to rind at later stages, including transcription factors from families known to be involved in the regulation of sugar accumulation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 41</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">42</a>). It is noteworthy that one bZIP gene, <i>Cla014572</i>, is downregulated during flesh development and contains the sucrose-controlled upstream open reading frame (SC-uORF) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). It was recently reported that transgenic plants constitutively expressing the tobacco SC-uORF containing the bZIP gene <i>tbz17</i> but lacking its SC-uORF had increased sugar concentrations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Thalor, S.K. et al. Deregulation of sucrose-controlled translation of a bZIP-type transcription factor results in sucrose accumulation in leaves. PLoS ONE 7, e33111 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2774">49</a></sup>. Therefore, our analysis is consistent with a role for <i>Cla014572</i> as a key regulator of sugar accumulation during fruit development.</p><p>MADS-box genes, such as <i>MADS-RIN</i> (also known as <i>LeMADS-RIN</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Vrebalov, J. et al. A MADS-box gene necessary for fruit ripening at the tomato ripening-inhibitor (rin) locus. Science 296, 343â346 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2790">50</a></sup> and <i>TAGL1</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Vrebalov, J. et al. Fleshy fruit expansion and ripening are regulated by the tomato SHATTERPROOF gene TAGL1. Plant Cell 21, 3041â3062 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2796">51</a>) in tomato, have been reported to regulate the fruit expansion and ripening processes. Phylogenetic analysis of watermelon, cucumber and <i>Arabidopsis</i> MADS-box transcription factors, together with <i>MADS-RIN</i> and <i>TAGL1</i>, identified two MADS-box transcription factors from watermelon in each of the <i>RIN</i> and <i>AGL1</i> clades (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>). These four genes (<i>Cla000691</i> and <i>Cla010815</i> in the <i>RIN</i> clade and <i>Cla009725</i> and <i>Cla019630</i> in the <i>AGL1</i> clade) are among the most highly expressed MADS-box transcription factors during fruit development (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 43</a>). Notably, unlike <i>MADS-RIN</i>, which is highly expressed only in ripening fruits, both <i>Cla000691</i> and <i>Cla010815</i> are highly expressed throughout fruit development, indicating they could have evolved to participate in other functions in addition to ripening. It is noteworthy in this regard that close banana and strawberry homologs of <i>MADS-RIN</i> also show expression and/or functional activities that extend beyond the ripening fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Elitzur, T., Vrebalov, J., Giovannoni, J.J., Goldschmidt, E.E. &amp; Friedman, H. The regulation of MADS-box gene expression during ripening of banana and their regulatory interaction with ethylene. J. Exp. Bot. 61, 1523â1535 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2858">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Seymour, G.B. et al. A SEPALLATA gene is involved in the development and ripening of strawberry (Fragaria Ã ananassa Duch.) fruit, a non-climacteric tissue. J. Exp. Bot. 62, 1179â1188 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2861">53</a></sup>. The expression profiles of <i>Cla009725</i> and <i>Cla019630</i> during fruit development are similar to that of <i>TAGL1</i>, which consistent with their potential roles in regulating fruit expansion and ripening<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Vrebalov, J. et al. Fleshy fruit expansion and ripening are regulated by the tomato SHATTERPROOF gene TAGL1. Plant Cell 21, 3041â3062 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2874">51</a></sup>.</p><p>Citrulline is a nonessential amino acid produced from glutamine and has various benefits to health and athletic performance. Its name is derived from <i>citrullus</i>, the Latin word for watermelon, from which it was first isolated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Wada, M. Ãber citrullin, eine neue aminosÃ¤ure im presssaft der wassermelone, Citrullus vulgaris Schrad. Biochem. Z. 224, 420 (1930)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2885">54</a></sup>. Watermelon flesh and rind serve as a natural source of citrulline, and its abundance increases substantially during fruit maturation but then declines as the fruit becomes over-ripe (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>). On the basis of our annotation of the watermelon genome, we identified 14 genes in the citrulline metabolic pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>).
