Compared to the <i>Arabidopsis</i> citrulline metabolic pathway, this pathway in watermelon has undergone expansion in the arginosuccinase and arginosuccinate synthase families. Both are involved in converting citrulline to <span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-arginine. We found an arginosuccinase and two arginosuccinate synthase genes to be highly downregulated during watermelon flesh development (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 44</a>). Thus, citrulline accumulation in the maturing fruit flesh is probably a result of decreased activities of citrulline degradation.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The draft watermelon genome sequence presented here represents an important resource for plant research and crop genetic improvement and also supports further evolutionary and comparative genomics studies of the <i>Cucurbitaceae</i>. The evolutionary scenario outlined for the cucurbits provides a clearly described series of genetic phenomena underlying a modern plant genome and yields new insights into the events that underlie the transition from ancestral chromosomes to modern chromosome architecture. Genome resequencing of representative watermelon accessions has provided a large source of haplotype data with great potential for genome manipulation, trait discovery and allele mining. Insights regarding the genetic diversity and population structure of watermelon accessions, as well as chromosome regions and genes under human selection, will shape future efforts in watermelon genetic research and breeding. The unique metabolic and regulatory networks in developing watermelon fruit identified from our functional genomics study represent an initial genomics-enabled milestone for the understanding and genetic improvement of crucial nutritional attributes, including sugar and amino acid contents. In addition, genomic resources that are available for both watermelon and cucumber greatly enhance the capacity to investigate at the whole-plant level the phloem-based vascular signaling systems that function to integrate developmental and physiological processes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">URLs.</h3><p>Watermelon genome database, <a href="http://www.icugi.org/">http://www.icugi.org/</a> and <a href="http://www.iwgi.org/">http://www.iwgi.org/</a>; FAO Statistics database, <a href="http://faostat.fao.org/">http://faostat.fao.org/</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Sequencing and assembly of the 97103 genome.</h3><p>Paired-end and mate-pair Illumina libraries were prepared following the manufacturer's instructions. Additional steps, including DNA circularization, digestion of linear DNA, fragmentation of circularized DNA and purification of biotinylated DNA, were performed before adaptor ligation for mate-pair libraries with insert size â¥2 kb. All libraries were sequenced on the Illumina GAII system using standard Illumina protocols. Raw Illumina reads were first processed by removing low-quality reads, adaptor sequences and possible contaminated reads of bacterial and viral origin. The clean reads were then assembled using SOAP<i>denovo</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265â272 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2960">55</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Transposable element annotation.</h3><p>Repeat sequences were first identified <i>de novo</i> from the genome assembly using PILER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Edgar, R.C. &amp; Myers, E.W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21 (suppl. 1), i152â158 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2978">56</a></sup> and RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Price, A.L., Jones, N.C. &amp; Pevzner, P.A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21 (suppl. 1), i351â358 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2982">57</a></sup>. LTR retrotransposons were identified by LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265â268 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d36135e2986">58</a></sup> with default parameters. We then used RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) and the known repbase library (<a href="http://www.girinst.org/repbase/index.html">http://www.girinst.org/repbase/index.html</a>) to find transposable element repeats in the assembled genome. Transposable elements were then classified as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275â1281 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3005">14</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Gene prediction and functional annotation.</h3><p>The repeat-masked watermelon genome was used for gene predictions. AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19 (suppl. 2), ii215â225 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3020">59</a></sup> and GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Majoros, W.H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3024">60</a></sup> were used for <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction. We also aligned watermelon EST and complementary DNA (cDNA) sequences onto the genome assembly using BLAT (identity â¥0.98 and coverage â¥0.95) to derive spliced alignments. Protein sequences of six plants (<i>Arabidopsis</i>, cucumber, poplar, rice, papaya and grape) were then aligned onto the watermelon genome using TBLASTN at an <i>E</i> value cutoff of 1 Ã 10<sup>â5</sup>, and the homologous genome sequences were then aligned against the matching proteins using GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3040">61</a></sup> for accurate spliced alignments. Outputs of the three methods described above (<i>ab initio</i>, cDNA and EST mapping and homology mapping) were integrated using GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3047">62</a></sup> to produce the consensus gene models. Functions of the predicted watermelon genes were assigned using AHRD (Automated assignment of Human Readable Descriptions) as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="The Tomato Genome Sequencing Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3051">63</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">FISH analysis.</h3><p>FISH analysis of unassembled repeat units similar to centromeres, telomeres and rDNAs in the 97103 genome and 45S and 5S rDNAs in the genomes of the 20 watermelon accessions were performed according to Ren <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ren, Y. et al. A high resolution genetic map anchoring scaffolds of the sequenced watermelon genome. PLoS ONE 7, e29453 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3069">10</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Analysis of cucurbit genome evolution.</h3><p>A method for the identification of orthologous regions between plant genomes, on the basis of integrative sequence alignment criteria combined with a statistical validation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Salse, J. et al. Improved criteria and comparative genomics tool provide new insights into grass paleogenomics. Brief. Bioinform. 10, 619â630 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3082">64</a></sup>, was used to unravel the <i>Cucurbitaceae</i> evolutionary paleohistory using watermelon genome information and results from a previous paleogenomics analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Salse, J. et al. Reconstruction of monocotelydoneous proto-chromosomes reveals faster evolution in plants than in animals. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 14908â14913 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3089">65</a></sup>. The duplication event in the watermelon genome was dated following the method of Murat <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Murat, F. et al. Ancestral grass karyotype reconstruction unravels new mechanisms of genome shuffling as a source of plant evolution. Genome Res. 20, 1545â1557 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3096">66</a></sup>. The syntenic relationships between watermelon, cucumber, melon and grape were analyzed using alignment parameters and statistical tests following the method of Salse <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Salse, J. et al. Improved criteria and comparative genomics tool provide new insights into grass paleogenomics. Brief. Bioinform. 10, 619â630 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3104">64</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome resequencing, read mapping and SNP and small indel calling.</h3><p>Paired-end Illumina libraries were prepared following the manufacturer's instructions and sequenced on an Illumina GAII system. We sequenced 44, 75 or 90 bp at each end. The Illumina paired-end reads from each watermelon accession were aligned to the reference 97103 genome sequences using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3119">67</a></sup>. For shorter reads (44 bp), we used the paired-end mapping mode of BWA and only kept reads with mapping quality &gt;16. For longer reads (75 or 90 bp), we used the single-end mapping mode of BWA and only kept reads that were uniquely mapped to the reference genome. After mapping, SNPs and small indels (1â5 bp) were identified on the basis of the mpileup files generated by SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence alignment/map (SAM) format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3123">68</a></sup>. Genotypes supported by at least two reads and with allele frequency â¥0.3 were assigned to each genomic position. Only homozygous SNPs and small indels were accepted.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Identification of new genes.</h3><p>Unmapped reads from the ten modern cultivated and ten semiwild and wild watermelon accessions were each pooled. The two pools of unmapped reads were assembled separately into contigs using SOAP<i>denovo</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265â272 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3137">55</a></sup>. Contigs shorter than 2 kb were discarded. New genes were predicted from the assembled contigs using AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19 (suppl. 2), ii215â225 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3141">59</a></sup> and then compared against the NCBI nr database using BLASTP.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Nucleotide diversity and selective sweep detection.</h3><p>Two standard estimates of the scaled mutation rate, <i>Î¸w</i>, the proportion of segregating sites, and <i>Ï</i>, the average pairwise nucleotide diversity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Tajima, F. Evolutionary relationship of DNA sequences in finite populations. Genetics 105, 437â460 (1983)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3159">25</a></sup>, were used to characterize nucleotide diversity among the examined watermelon populations. A sliding window approach was used to calculate the <i>Î¸w</i> and <i>Ï</i> along all 11 watermelon chromosomes with a window size of 50 kb and a step size of 10 kb. To identify potential selective sweeps, we compared nucleotide diversity between populations of modern cultivars (<i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>vulgaris</i>) and semiwild accessions (<i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>mucosospermus</i>). Genome regions with highest (top 1%) genetic diversity (<i>Ï</i><sub><i>mucosospermus</i></sub>/<i>Ï</i><sub><i>vulgaris</i></sub>) were identified as potential selective sweeps. We also identified regions with smallest (top 1%) <i>Ï</i><sub><i>mucosospermus</i></sub>/<i>Ï</i><sub><i>vulgaris</i></sub> values to serve as negative controls. GO terms enriched in genes from the selective sweeps were identified with GO::TermFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Boyle, E.I. et al. GO:TermFinder: open source software for accessing Gene Ontology information and finding significantly enriched Gene Ontology terms associated with a list of genes. Bioinformatics 20, 3710â3715 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3207">69</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Phylogenetic relationship and population structure analyses.</h3><p>In these analyses, we used a subset of <span class="stix">â¼</span>1.46 million SNPs that had information in all 21 watermelon accessions. A neighbor-joining tree was constructed using PHYLIP (<a href="http://evolution.genetics.washington.edu/phylip.html">http://evolution.genetics.washington.edu/phylip.html</a>) and MEGA5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: Molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731â2739 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3225">70</a>) was used to display the tree. PCA was performed with EIGENSOFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Price, A.L. et al. Principal components analysis corrects for stratification in genome-wide association studies. Nat. Genet. 38, 904â909 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3229">71</a></sup>. We further used FRAPPE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Tang, H., Peng, J., Wang, P. &amp; Risch, N.J. Estimation of individual admixture: analytical and study design considerations. Genet. Epidemiol. 28, 289â301 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3233">29</a></sup> to investigate the population structure with 10,000 iterations and the number of clusters (<i>K</i>) of 2â5.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Comparative transcriptome analysis of watermelon, cucumber and pumpkin phloem sap and vascular tissues.</h3><p>Phloem sap and vascular tissues were collected from the main stem located in the central region of 6-week-old watermelon (cv. 97103), cucumber (cv. Chinese Long) and pumpkin (cv. Big max) plants. Paired-end and single-end strand-specific RNA-Seq libraries were prepared from these tissues according to Zhong <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Zhong, S. et al. High-throughput Illumina strand-specific RNA sequencing library preparation. Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. 8, 940â949 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3251">44</a></sup> and sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2000 system. Three independent biological replica samples were prepared. RNA-Seq reads were first aligned to rRNA sequences using Bowtie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Langmead, B., Trapnell, C., Pop, M. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. Ultrafast and memory-efficient alignment of short DNA sequences to the human genome. Genome Biol. 10, R25 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3255">72</a></sup> to eliminate possible rRNA sequence contamination. The resulting watermelon and cucumber reads were aligned to the corresponding genomes using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105â1111 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3259">73</a></sup>. Pumpkin RNA-Seq reads were <i>de novo</i> assembled into contigs using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Grabherr, M.G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3267">74</a></sup>, and then reads were aligned back to the assembled contigs using Bowtie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Langmead, B., Trapnell, C., Pop, M. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. Ultrafast and memory-efficient alignment of short DNA sequences to the human genome. Genome Biol. 10, R25 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3271">72</a></sup>. The count of mapped reads for each gene from each sample was then derived and normalized to fragments per kilobase of exon model per million mapped reads (FPKM). We defined vascular transcripts as those with FPKM â¥ 2 in all three biological replica samples and phloem sap transcripts as those with FPKM â¥ 2 in all three biological replica samples and enriched with at least twofold higher levels in phloem sap compared to vascular bundle (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2470#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Fruit transcriptome sequencing and analysis.</h3><p>Fruit flesh and rind tissues of 97103 were collected at four developmental stages: 10, 18, 26 and 34 days after pollination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Guo, S. et al. Characterization of transcriptome dynamics during watermelon fruit development: sequencing, assembly, annotation and gene expression profiles. BMC Genomics 12, 454 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3287">75</a></sup>. RNA extraction and strand-specific RNA-Seq library preparations were performed as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Zhong, S. et al. High-throughput Illumina strand-specific RNA sequencing library preparation. Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. 8, 940â949 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3291">44</a></sup>, and RNA-Seq libraries were sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2000 system. Two independent biological replica samples were prepared. RNA-Seq reads were first processed to remove rRNA sequence contamination. The resulting reads were aligned to the watermelon 97103 genome sequences using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105â1111 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3295">73</a></sup>. After alignment, for each watermelon gene model, the count of mapped reads from each sample was derived and normalized to FPKM. Differentially expressed genes during flesh or rind development and between the flesh and rind at the same stages were identified using the LIMMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Smyth, G.K. Linear models and empirical Bayes methods for assessing differential expression in microarray experiments. Stat. Appl. Genet. Molec. Biol. 3, Article 3 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3299">76</a></sup> and DESeq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Anders, S. &amp; Huber, W. Differential expression analysis for sequence count data. Genome Biol. 11, R106 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3303">77</a></sup> packages, respectively. Raw <i>P</i> values of multiple tests were corrected using the FDR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. R. Stat. Soc. B 57, 289â300 (1995)." href="/articles/ng.2470#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d36135e3311">78</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section>
